This report was submitted in October, the chief recommendations were, that the scavenging gang should commence work at 3 a.m., instead of 6 a.m., so that the greater part-of the main roads should be dealt with before the commencement of the ordinary day's traffic; that the main streets should be dealt with daily, and the other streets twice, or three times, a week, according to circumstances. The early commencement of work necessitated the opening of the shoot at an earlier hour by the wharf owner than was arranged in the contract; the Committee therefore agreed to pay to Mr. Murrell the sum of £1 per week during the winter months. The collection of dust was also overhauled, and it was anticipated that a bi-weekly collection of dust might be obtained in the greater part of 61 the parish. After due consideration by the Committee, it was decided that the above recommendations should be put in force, and on the 5th of December a commencement was made under the new system, and up to the present time it has resulted in much greater efficiency with no additional cost; the streets have been cleansed oftener, and the dust is collected from over three-fourths of the parish twice weekly, and the remaining portion once weekly, the crowded and poorer class of property receiving most attention, and is dealt with in the bi-weekly collection. The Vestry have decided to provide waterproof jackets, overalls, and sou'wester' for the men employed in scavenging and dusting, so that the men may be better able to do their work during the wet days, and when it is possible to more thoroughly sweep clean the roads. ELECTEIC LIGHTING TEENCHES. A great amount of trench work has been carried out in this parish during the year, and it has been considerably added to by the County of London and Brush Provincial Electric Lighting Co., who commenced, about the beginning of October, to lay their cables in Old Kent Eoad. They then proceeded along New Kent Road, Tabard Street, Long Lane, Borough High Street, Newington Causeway, Borough Eoad, and Blackfriars Eoad. In connection with the above work, on the section crossing Borough High Street, from Long Lane to the corner of Marshalsea Eoad, I found they were diverting from the plan submitted and approved, both in line and as to the number of mains. On the 14th February, after drawing the attention of the Foreman in charge of the work to this, I wrote to the Manager on the matter. On the 16th, not having received any reply to my letter, I again visited the work, and found they were making further deviations, and proceeding along the south side of Marshalsea Eoad, over which they had no power. I therefore gave orders that the work must not proceed further, and had the ground filled up. From that date, until an injunction was granted to the Yestry against the Company, I had the ground watched day and night, to prevent any further openings. LIGHTING. During the year several lamps have been fixed in the courts and side-streets, and a notable addition in lighting has been made in 62 Borough High Street, near Marshalsea Road, by the erection of a column, and the fixing of two globe lanterns, with two No. 4 incandescent burners in each; two columns with lanterns containing incandescent burners have also been fixed near the entrance to the Free Library. The question of lighting that part of the parish supplied by the South Metropolitan Gas Company, with incandescent burners is at present under consideration, and several lamps in Borough Road have been fitted, to test the effect. UNDERGROUND CONVENIENCES. During the year the construction of the following underground conveniences, &c., in various parts of the parish have been in hand:— In Old Kent Road, near the junction of New Kent Road and Bermondsey New Road, a convenience, with accommodation for both men and women, has been provided, the provision being, in the men's portion, eight stalls, seven w.c.'s (one of which is free), and two lavatory basins, for which hot and cold water is provided; and in the women's part, four w.c.'s (one a free one), and one lavatory. This work was carried out by Messrs. Finch & Co., and was opened for public use on the 27th February. The contract amount for this was £2,049, and the actual cost ,£2072 15s. 8d. In Borough Road, at the end next to St. George's Circus, a convenience for men only has been constructed, the provision being ten stalls, six w.c.'s (one of which is free), and one lavatory basin; provision is also made here for hot and cold water. This was opened on 16th March, the work being carried out by Messrs. Finch & Co. The contract amount was £1,659, and the actual cost £1650 5s. 11d. At the Newington Causeway end of Borough Road an under- ground urinal has been constructed, to take the place of the surface urinal removed at the time of the Jubilee. This also has been built b) Messrs. Finch, and will, I expect, be ready for opening before the end of March. The contract in this case was £879, and the actual cost £824 11s. 4d. in November, a contract was let to Messrs. Smith & Sons, for the construction of a ladies' convenience on a site behind the Free Library; but very little progress has been made with this. Terra cotta dressings are being introduced into the work, so as to be in 63 harmony with the Library; this has been the chief cause of delay, but it is now expected that the work will be completed early in June. The provisions are three w.c's and two washhand basins. The contract amount of this work is £363. GENERAL. The work of laying out the Paragon and Portland Place is well in hand, and it is expected they will be ready for opening early in June. The work here has been carried out jointly by the Vestry and the Metropolitan Public Gardens Association, the Vestry providing and fixing the railings, gates, hydrants, paving the footpaths, &c., and the Metropolitan Gardens Association laying out the grounds, providing the trees, and planting. We have now planted in the parish 421 trees—during the year, 37 trees either died or were knocked down, these were replaced, and are, generally, going on satisfactorily. The church-clock, up to the present, has been lighted by gas, and has not been giving satisfaction—it was therefore decided, after receiving estimates from the Surveyor, as to the cost of wiring and lighting, to have it lighted by electricity. This was turned on for the first time on the 2nd of March, with satisfactory results, and it is anticipated that the cost of electricity will not exceed that previously paid for gas. A refuge has been fixed in the centre of the Borough High Street, near Marshalsea Road, and it has been greatly appreciated by those crossing this busy thoroughfare, and it has also been of great assistance in regulating the traffic. At the request of the Metropolitan Drinking Fountain Association, the cleansing of the horse-troughs erected in this parish by the Association has been taken over, and is now carried out by your staff. I am, Ladies and Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, ARTHUR HARRISON, A. M. Inst. C.E., Surveyor to the Vestry. ABSTRACT OF THE ACCOUNTS OF THE VESTRY, of the Parish of Saint George the Martyr. H. PATTEB8ON, A.S.A.A, Vestry Accountant. AUDITOR'S REPORTS. 116 AUDITOR'S REPORTS. 30, Moorgate Street, London, E.C. 19th June, 1899. J. A. Johnson, Esq., The Yestry Hall, Borough Road, S,E Dear Sir, We herewith beg to hand you the following Accounts relating to the Yestry of St. George the Martyr, duly certified by us viz. : — General and Sewers Rates, Expenditure and Income Accounts, Balance Sheet and Capital Account. The Income and Expenditure Accounts show balances as follows:— In favour of the General Rate In favour of the Sewers Rate £ s. d. 5141 9 6 3323 4 8 £8464 14 2 At March 25th, 1898, the balances were :— In favour of the General Rate In favour of the Sewers Rate £ s. d. . 2599 6 4 . 4730 7 4 £7329 13 8 It will thus be seen that the Yestry's position has improved during the last completed financial year, to the extent of <£1135 0s. 6d. 117 The Rate Collection for 1898-9, as compared with 1897-8, is shewn in the following statement:— 1897—8. 1898—9. Increase. Decrease. £ s. d. £ s. d. £ s. d. £ s. d. General Eate 35893 2 8 35918 8 11 25 6 3 — Sewers Eate 1680 2 0 1186 15 2 — 493 6 10 Total £37573 4 8 £37105 4 1 — £46£ 0 7 The Contribution under the Equalisation of Rates Act, for the year under review was £5753 9s. 7d., as against £5672 Is. 4d. in 1897 to 1898. The Stocks of materials on hand, we are glad to say, agree in a much more satisfactory manner with the Stocks Ledger balances than in the previous year. We have pleasure also in saying that the general keeping of the books show a marked improvement. We have audited the Overseers Cash Book and Rate Books (General and Sewers) and also the Churchwardens Charities Accounts. We shall be pleased to give you any further information in our power. We are, Dear Sir, Yours faithfully, HART BROS., TIBBETTS, HEIRON, & CO. 118 STATEMENT of ALLOWANCE of the AUDITORS. (18 & 19 Vict., cap. 120 s. 196). ESTIMATES FOR THE YEAR ENDING MARCH 25TH, 1900, AND SUMMARY OF INCOME & EXPENDITURE FOR THE YEAR ENDED MARCH 25TH, 1898. » CHAS. H. PATTERSON, A.S.A.A., Accountant. 122 Report of the Finance Committee. To the VESTRY OF ST. GEORGE THE MARTYR, SOUTHWARK. Your Committee beg to submit the detailed Estimates of the various Committees, showing the Expenditure under the General Eate and Sewers Rate for the Financial Year ending March 25th. 1900, as follows £ General Rate 34,558 Sewers Rate 1,873 £36,431 Having in view the Balance which will be standing to the Credit of the General Rate Account on the 25th March, 1899, your Committee are of opinion that the amount to be raised by General Rate should be reduced by the sum of £2000. The Committee accordingly recommend:— 1. That the Estimates be approved. 2. That an Order be made on the Overseers of the Poor, and sealed, to raise and pay over to the Vestry the sum of £32,558 for the purposes of defraying the expenses of the Vestry properly chargeable to the General Rate for the year ending 25th March, 1900, and that the aforesaid sum of £32,558 be payable as stated below. 3. That an Order be made on the Overseers of the Poor, and sealed, to raise and pay over to the Vestry the sum of £1,873 for the purpose of defraying the expenses of the Vestry properly chargeable to the Sewers Rate for the year ending 25th March, 1900, and that the aforesaid sum of £1,873 be payable as stated below. On or before June 24th, 1899, Sept. 29th, 1899. Dec. 25th, 1899. March 25th, 1900. Totals. £ s. d £ s. d. £ s. d. £ s. d. £ s. d. GEORGE THE MARTYR, SOUTHWARD For the Year Ended 25th March, 1899. The Overseers at the termination of their first year of office, after appointment by the Vestry, beg to report as follows:— COMPOUNDING FOR RATES. In accordance with the resolution of the 21st February, 1898, the Overseers adopted a new scale of allowances to owners of property, compounding for the payment of rates. The Overseers caused notices to be given to the whole of the owners who were then receiving the usual allowance of 25 per cent. on rates payable by them under Compounding Agreements. The notices were sent out immediately prior to the 25th March, 1898, and terminated at three months after that date. The Overseers also forwarded to the owners a circular asking them to enter into new Compounding Agreements on the reduced basis of commission adopted by the Vestry, viz.:— Model Dwellings rated at a sum not exceeding £20, a commission of 15 per cent. on the amount of Kates received from the owner, Small or Tenement houses rated at a sum not exceeding £10, a like commission of 20 per cent. Small or Tenement houses rated at a sum exceeding £10, but not exceeding £15, a like commission of 15 per cent. 138 A few of the owners raised objections to the scale as being inadequate to meet their losses on empty property, particularly where property was old and dilapidated, but the scheme was accepted by a large majority of those concerned. Before the end of the year, 204 Agreements had been entered into by owners, practically representing the whole of the available compounding property within the Parish. HALF YEARLY RATES. In connection with the re-adjustment of the Compounding Scale it was felt by the Board that the custom of making the rates quarterly and having the Rate Books re-printed for that purpose every three months, was cumbrous and contrary to the practice adopted in the majority of the larger Parishes throughout the Metropolis, and on the 2nd May, the Board determined that the rates should, in future, be made half yearly instead of quarterly. The alteration commenced from the 29th September, 1898. RATE BOOKS, DEMAND NOTES, ETC. The Board deemed the occasion of making half-yearly rates and the alteration of the Compounding commission a suitable time for the re-organisation of the Rate Books and the Demand and Receipt Notes for the Collectors. Alter a sub-Committee had carefully enquired into the matter, it was determined to alter the form of Rate Books so as to emboely two quarters' rates in one book. Another and more important amendment was made with regard to the order in which the names were set forth in the books. It had been the practice hitherto to place the names in the order in which it was most convenient for the Collectors to call upon the ratepayers, regardless of street or alphabetical order, the result being that the premises in some streets were scattered over a large number of pages throughout the Rate Hooks rendering it extremely difficult to ascertain whether the whole of the premises in such streets had been properly assessed. Having in view the provisions of the Registration Acts and the necessity for more readily finding the premises on the Rate Books, the Overseers deemed it advisable that in future the premises should bo entered in the books in alphabetical street order. It 139 was at the same time decided to introduce the modern practice of dinting the amount of rate payable, on the Demand and Receipt Notes his system being found to secure greater accuracy as well as being a check on fraud. REGISTRATION. At the Revision Court for the year 1898, on the accounts of the Overseers being presented to the Revising Barrister (Mr. Michael Moloney), he objected to the charges made therein of £60 for the services of the Vestry Clerk and of £36 15s. for clerical assistance rendered by the clerks in the office, on the ground that these amounts were not to be paid to the persons whose names they were set against, but were ultimately to be transferred to the Vestry's account, the Vestry Clerk and clerks being paid inclusive salaries to cover the work of Eegistration. He therefore struck the amounts out of the account, and they will not be received by the Vestry. AUDIT OF COLLECTORS' ACCOUNTS. The serious irregularities which occurred in connection with the accounts of the Collector of Ward 1, who was removed by the Vestry from his office, were on the Audit of the Overseers' accounts for the half year ending 29th September, 1898, commented upon by the District Auditor, who expressed the opinion that the counterfoils of the Overseers Receipt Books should be frequently compared with the Bank Pass Books and the Collectors Paying-in-Books. After giving very careful consideration to the suggestion, the Overseers were of opinion that a weekly Audit of the counterfoils of the Collectors Receipt Books should be made, and it was felt that the best method of having this work carried out was to employ Professional Auditors, which would secure a more independent audit than appointing an extra clerk for the purpose. The General Rate Accounts are audited from time to time on behalf of the Vestry, by Messrs. Hart Brothers, Tibbetts & Co., and after ascertaining that that firm would undertake the Audit of the Poor Rate Accounts weekly, at the sum of thirty guineas per annum, the Overseers on the 6th February, 1899, agreed to employ them for this purpose. 140 RATES. During the year the Assessments of 1268 premises have been under review by the Overseers. Of these, old assessments have been altered representing a Gross Assessable Value of £34,162 and Rateable Value of £28,025, producing an increase of Gross Value £4,738 and Rateable Value £4,165 on the previous assessments, while new assessments to the extent of a Gross Assessable Value of £3,735 and Rateable Value £3,093 have been added. The total increase in the Gross and Rateable Value of the Parish for the year is therefore Gross £8,473, Rateable £7,258. DISORDERLY HOUSES. The Board have during the past year actively proceeded with the work of suppressing disorderly houses. Several houses have been watched and in tour cases proceedings were taken. The prosecutions were conducted by the Solicitor and Clerk to the Overseers, with the result that in three cases heavy tines were inflicted, and in the fourth the defendant was sent to prison for four months. 141 Communications were received from the Clerk to the Overseers of Lambeth in December, 1898, suggesting that it was desirable to convene a Conference of Overseers with reference to the above natter, with a view of obtaining an alteration of the law, transferring to the Police authorities the duty of suppressing brothels. The Board passed a resolution to the effect that, in their opinion the law as it stands, is sufficient to suppress disorderly houses, and that the Board have never during recent years paid infonners fees in consequence of expeditious steps having been taken in this Parish for the suppression of the nuisance, and informing the Lambeth Overseers that the Board were prepared to appoint Representatives to the Conference, but such Representatives would be instructed to oppose any legislation on the lines proposed. For the Rate Collectors, see p.p. 143, 145, 146 and 147. 157 REPORT OF THE Commissioners for Public Libraries AND MUSEUMS, For the year ending 25th March, 1899. The Public Library Commissioners have to report, that during the past year the work of erecting, furnishing and fitting up the Public Library has been completed. LIBRARIAN. One of the first matters calling for the attention of the Commissioners at the commencement of the year, was the question of appointing a suitable person as Chief Librarian. Advertisements were issued for a Librarian at a salary of £160 per annum, rising by annual increments of £10 to £200 per annum with house, coals and gas so soon as the building should be completed. Eighty-four (84) applications were received, and Mr. Thomas Aldred, the Chief Librarian at Barrow-in-Furness, was appointed to the post, and he commenced his duties on the 25th May, 1898. LADIES' CONVENIENCE. An arrangement was arrived at, and a formal agreement dated the 15th October, 1898, was entered into between the Yestry and the Commissioners for the letting to the former Authority of a small plot of ground on the westerly side of the Library for the erection of a Ladies Convenience, at the rent of £5 per annum. BOOKS. Immediately after the appointment of the Librarian, the Committee proceeded to the selection of suitable books for stocking the 158 Library, and were very largely assisted in the matter by the Members of the Books Committee, several of whom are not Commissioners. A sum of .£850 was set apart to be spent in the purchase of books, and there are now 6,472 volumes in the Library. The Commissioners made appeals to various institutions and persons for gifts of books, and these were in many case's responded to in a generous spirit. Application was made to the Lords Commissioners of Her Majesty's Treasury for copies of the Record Office publications, and 208 volumes were subsequently received from that Department. A similar application was made to the Authorities of the British Museum for their publications; but the Trustees stated that they would be prepared to consider the application when the Library had been established in a permanent building. The application will accordingly be shortly renewed. The method adopted in purchasing a large number of the books was that of sending round lists of books required to the second-hand booksellers throughout the kingdom and inviting quotations. These prices were subsequently examined by the Librarian and agreed to by the Commissioners. The majority of the volumes in the Library was purchased in this manner, and the remainder were obtained at prices from which considerably more than the usual discount was allowed. Tenders were also obtained for binding works published in paper covers, such as music, &c. BOOKS OF ACCOUNT, LIBRARY SPECIALITIES, ETC. The Commissioners deemed it desirable to obtain Tenders for these matters, with the result that the prices of the following firms were accepted:— Books of Account—Mr. T. Cornell. Library Specialities—Library Supply Company. Typewriting Machine—Hammond Typewriting Company. FITTINGS. The Commissioners obtained Tenders for the necessary book racks, tables, newspaper stands and counter from various firms, and Messrs. Wake & Dean, whose business is carried on in this Parish, obtained the contract. 159 RULES. In view of the early opening of the Library the Commissioners on the 12th October, 1898, adopted rules as to the opening, closing, admission of persons and borrowing of books in the various departments of the Library. CATALOGUE. Immediately a sufficient number of works had been purchased, the Librarian proceeded with the preparation of the Catalogue of the Library, and in November, 1898, the Commissioners obtained tenders for the printing of the work, and Mr. T. Cornell's tender being the lowest, was accepted. OPENING OF LIBRARY. The formal opening of the Library took place on the 8th February, 1899. The ceremony was performed by the Right. Hon. James Bryce. D.C.L., M.P., who delivered an admirable address on that occasion. Mr. J. Passmore Edwards, whose handsome donation of £5,000 has now been fully paid, was also present. DESCRIPTION OF LIBRARY BUILDING. In order to afford the Vestry a full description of the Library Building, the Commissioners submit the following statement prepared by the Architect. The building stands some twenty feet back from the pavement in the Borough Road, and the space thus available is turfed. This arrangement greatly enhances the architectural effect of the building, and also removes the reading rooms further from the noise of the passing traffic. The building consists of a basement, ground floor, and first floor, with an attic floor over the portion assigned to the Librarian's residence. The basement extends over the whole area of the building, being well lighted by an area which completely surrounds it. In the basement are the heating chamber and the coal cellars; the rest of the space is available for the storage of books, and for repairs, and unpacking, &c. 160 The ground floor is placed about three feet above the level of the pavement. On entering, one reaches the Ladies' Room on the right hand side, and the News Room on the left hand side, the public portion of the Lending Library being immediately in front. The Boys' Room has a door opening into the News Room, but it is proposed that the usual means of access shall be through the Lending Library, thus enabling the staff to have full control over those who use the room. The Public Rooms are all under the direct supervision of the staff in the Lending Library, as is also the Entrance Hall. On the first floor is the Reference Library and Magazine Room, with Book Store attached. A spiral staircase from the Book Store communicates with the Lending Library and basement below. This enables the staff to reach one department from another, with great facility. Adjoining the Reference Library is the Committee Room. There is a separate entrance for the Librarian's staff, and this entrance also leads to the Librarian's residence, which comprises— Drawing-room, dining-room, and three bedrooms, bath-room, kitchen, scullery, &c. The exterior facings of the walls are of red brick, with terra cotta dressings, cornices, mouldings, and enrichments, the roof being covered with slates. Over the entrance is a panel bearing the inscription "Passmore Edwards Public Library." The rest of the space being filled in with a conventional Tree of Knowledge. The figure subject in the upper portion of the gable represents St. George and the Dragon, and the shields on either side of this bear the Cross of St. George and the Badge of the Vestry of St. George the Martyr, Southwark, respectively. Above the western window there is a panel representing "Truth holding the mirror up to Nature." The main staircase is of teak, the newels, balusters, and handrail being polished, while the doors and screens are of varnished pine, the wood being stained in two different tints. This treatment is also carried out to the wood panel ceiling of the Reference Library. Glass has been largely used, in order to provide as much light as possible throughout the building. 161 The Electric Light has been installed in the building, which is heated throughout by means of hot water pipes, with fixed Radiators. SUBSCRIPTIONS. The Commissioners made every effort to raise a sum during the year sufficient, along with the amount of rates receivable by them up to the 25th March, 1899, to cover not only the cost of erecting the Library, but also the purchase of the freehold of the land. They were, however, only able to obtain in promises and subscriptions the sum of £6,060, which leaves a sum approximating £3,900 still to be raised. The Commissioners are hoping to raise a further amount by subscriptions, but it is feared that it will eventually become necessary to obtain a loan towards the purchase of the freehold. ANNUAL ACCOUNTS. The Commissioners submit their accounts for the year ending 25th March, 1899, as audited by the District Auditor, from which it appears that the Income during the year (including balance from previous year) was £7,912 11s. 2d., the expenditure £5,597 6s. 10½d., and the balance standing to the credit of the Commissioners at the end of the year £2,315 4s. 3½d. ALEXANDER HAWKINS, Chairman of Commissioners. J. A. JOHNSON, Clerk to Commissioners. 162 163 PUBLIC LIBRARY COMMISSIONERS. Statement of Receipts and Expenditure. to the 25th March. 1899. INCOME. AYLMER Disinfector: WILLIAM THOMAS Vestry Hall Mortuary Attendant and Assistant Disinfector: JOSEPH H.PIKE Vestry Hall ANNUAL REPORT on the SANITARY CONDITION of the Parish of St. Beorge the Martyr, Southwark, FOR THE YEAR 1898, by the MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH. To the Vestry of the Parish of St. George the Martyr. sectionl—statistical. Ladies and Gentlemen, The Seventh Annual Report, which I now have the honour to submit to you, treats of the sanitary condition and environment, for the year 1898, of one of the most densely populated districts in London, and, indeed, for that matter, in the United Kingdom.* The following summary of facts will be found useful in approaching the various matters contained in this report:— * According to the 1896 census Whitechapel heads the list with 220 per acre, and St. George the Martyr stands next with 212. 6 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. ABEA _St. George's, Southwark, covers 2841/3 acres. The roads and streets, as returned to the end of 1898, measure 19 miles, 5 furlongs, 14 poles. Boundaries. The parish, in shape somewhat like a hatchet, merges upou no less than six other Sanitary Districts. It is bounded on the north by St. Saviour and St Olave, on the west by Lambeth, on the south by Newington and Camberwell, and on the east by Bermondsey. Geological Formation.—The subsoil of Southwark consists of porous " made " earth, sand, and gravel, of a depth of fifteen to twenty-five feet, overlying the London clay. Saline springs rise at times to the surface, and in certain excavated parts of the parish e.g., Theatre and Music Hall—necessitate the constant use of pumps. Elevation. The district is low-lying and flat, and lies at an average of half-afoot below the Trinity high-water mark of the Thames, as against a mean elevation for London of 47.5 feet above the same mark. Houses.—I am indebted to the Medical Officer of the Administrative County for a copy of the Superintendent Registrar's Summary, giving the numbers resident in the district on the 29th March, 189G. This summary—given under Table I.—shows also there were 5,512 inhabited and 657 uninhabited houses, which makes an average of 10 9 persons to a house. TABLE 1. London Census, 1896. Its Number of tenements is almost exactly double that of its inhabited houses.of the tenement occupiers, one-third live" in two rooms, and another third in a single room.There is no reason to alter the statement advanced Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 7 ???r several years past that about one in every fourteen of your population is born, ???ows up, lives, works, and often dies within the four walls of a one.roomed ???nement. Prom Table I. it will be seen that the number of males in the parish is some 200 ???excess of the females. This may, I think, be explained by the following facts:. ???the presence of a number of factories where men are employed either exclusively ???mainly; (b) the comparatively small number of domestic servants employed in the arish; (c) the presence of the Salvation Army Shelter, which attracts to the parish daily average of several hundred men—(in 1895 the senior district magistrate stimated the Salvation Army accommodation at 550 per night);—and (d) Bethlem ???ospital, in which there is an excess of male patients. Another point brought ???trongly into relief by Table I. is the fact that the division of the parish into sub. ???istricts affords a more equal grouping of inhabitants than a division by Wards. Thus the greatest difference between the individual sub.district population is ???,796, whereas in the Wards the extreme difference is 9,304. This disproportion has a somewhat important bearing on various matters of administration. Population.—The number of inhabitants for the whole parish, according to the Census of 29th March, 1896, was 60,278, as against 59,712 in the Census of 1891. In the middle of 1898 the figures estimated on the foregoing Census reached 60,466. The rate of increase was extremely low, compared with that of London generally. Births and Birth=Rate. Table II. gives the birth.rate per 1,000 per annum for the years 1895, 1896, 1897, and 1898 in each sub.district of the parish, in London, and in the thirty.three large English towns. TABLE II. Sub.District. Birth.rate per 1,000. 1895. 1896. 1897. 1898. Borough Road 38.2 39.4 37.5 39 6 London Road Kent Road 30.8 39.6 32.6 37.8 31.4 36.6 32.1 35.5 Whole Parish 36.0 36.6 350 35.4 London 30.4 30.4 29.9 29.5 33 large English Towns 31.6 30.7 30.6 30.3 It will be seen that the birth rate is 0.4 higher than that of the preceding year, and is considerably above that for the whole of London. The diminished rate of increase of population in the three sub.districts of the parish of St. George's is, therefore, not attri butable to a falling off in the number of persons born into the parish, but rather to a relatively high death.rate, and also, probably, to migration from the parish, since the death.rate is below the birth.rate in each sub.district. A reference to the section of the mortality of infants and children will reveal the fact that in the year 1898 nearly one.half, i.e., 45.4 of the total deaths in St. George's, Southwark, have taken place uuder five years of age. 8 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. The fact is notable that the birth-rate in St. George's is higher than in London and in the thirty-three large towns. This may, I think, be partly explained by th. fact that the limitation of families is carried into effect by the better-to-do classes rather than by the poor. Deaths and Death-Rate for 1898. 1,218 deaths were registered in the parish during the fifty-two weeks ending 31st December, 1898, giving an average of 23 4 per week. Of this total 185 were deaths of non-parishioners in hospitals and asylums within the district. In additioin 436 parishioners died in outside public institutions and extra metropolitan asylums There were, therefore, 1,469 deaths amongst your parishioners, equivalent to a??? average mortality of 24.3 per 1,000. The death-rate for all London in the same year was 18.7 per 1,000, which is in itself, according to many authorities, 2 7 more per 1,000 than should occur from "the mortality incident to human nature." In St. Georgo's then, the death toll"—as it has been aptly called—claims 5.6 persons out of every 1,000 in excess of an already excessive rate. On the latter estimate, that of the whole of London, there have been some 339 unnecessary deaths in St. George's Southwark, during the year 1898, and comparing it with the ideal estimate, the unnecessary deaths number no less than 504. In the forty-three metropolitan sanitary areas the lowest death-rates were 11.7 in Hampstead, 13.2 in St. George's, Hanover Square, 13 7 in Stoke Newington, 14 .4 in Wandsworth, 14 9 in Lee, and 15.3 in Lewisham (excluding Penge); the highea??? death-rates, on the other hand, were 23 6 in St. Saviour's, Southwark, 23.8 in Lime house, 24.3 in St. George's, Southwark, 24 8 in St. George-in-the-East, 24.9 in Holborn. and 25.7 in St. Luke's. The facts of your death-rate are briefly as follows:— TABLE III. Sub-District. No. of Deaths. Death-rate per 1,000. Deaths under 1 year to 1,000 Births. 1897. 1898. 1897. 1898. 1897. 1898. One is naturally led to enquire whether a further analysis of facts and figures will throw any light upon this state of affairs. On comparing the average death-rate in the three sub-districts for the years 1892-3-4-5-6-7-8 with that of 1898, we find:- Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health.—1898. 9 TABLE IV. Sub-District. Death-rate per 1,000. 1892 to 1898. 1898. Borough Road 31.4 28.4 London Road 20.6 21.4 Kent Road 22.3 24.2 Hence we see the heavy death-rate in the Borough Road Sub-District has somewhat decreased. During the past year the mortality in the Borough Road has been 7.0 over that of the London Road, and 4.2 over that of the Kent Road areas. The period over which these figures extend—seven years—and the actual number of deaths are both too small to allow of drawing any absolutely sound conclusions. Nevertheless, it may be noticed that the mortality of the Borough Road Sub-District is 95 higher than that of the thirty-three large English towns. Further, the figures, so far as they extend, show that the excessive mortality of the Borough Road SubDistrict is maintained from year to year, so that it appears to be due to inherent and not to accidental causes. The annual death-rate for 1898 has risen to 24.3, as against 236 in 1897. There is still room for large and permanent reduction in the death-rate of St. George the Martyr. Were it possible to eliminate the Borough Road Sub-District or to reduce its mortality to a level with that of the other sub-districts, the condition of St. George the Martyr, as a whole, would compare favourably with many districts much more happily situated, both as regards density of population and general healthiness of surroundings. If it be permissible to offer any observation upon the foregoing figures, and bearing in mind that comparisons of the kind are open to fallacies, it may be said that George the Martyr, Southwark, stands roughly with regard to its death-rate where London stood in the ten-yearly period 1851-60. At the same time the deathrate in both cases has shown a most satisfactory and uniform average decrease. Zymotic or Preventable Death-Rate. It may be noted that the term "zymotic" applies to the following seven diseasesSmall-pox, measles, scarlet fever, diphtheria, whooping cough, "fever" (ie. typhus, relapsing puerperal, simple continued and typhoid) and diarrhœa. I have called the above zymotic diseases "preventable," because I regard all communicable diseases, broadly speaking, as coming under that heading. Diarrhoea is a symptom as well as a specific disease, and, although not usually communicated from one person to another, it may be regarded as the almost invarable result of bad environment. At the same time, it must be clearly understood that a vast amount of preventable disease, such as that due to alcoholism, is not zymotic. In other words, while all zymotic disease is preventable, not all preventable diseases are zymotic. The corrected death-rate in St. George's, Southwark, from the seven principal zymotic diseases was 4.0 per 1,000 in 1898, as against 2.8 per 1.000 in the whole of London for the same period, and 2.9 in the thirty-three great towns of England and Wales. As in the calculation of the general death-rate, so with the zymotic death-rate, the deaths of non-pirishioners occurring within tho district have been excluded; on the other hand, tho deaths of parishioners in public institutions outside the parish have been included. Unless a proper distribution of these deaths be made, especially of those from zymotic diseases, no really trustworthy data can be secured upon which to calculate the rates of mortality. Among the forty-three sanitary areas the zymotic death-rate ranged from 0.88 in St.-Martin-in-the-Fields, 129 in St. George's, Hanover Square, 1.40 in Hampstead, 1.51 in St. James, Westminster, 1.56 in Stoke Newington, and 1.76 in Strand, to 3.90 in Bethual Green,3.95 in Shoreditch, 4 0 in St. George's, Southwark, 4.04 in St. Luke, 4.13 in Woolwich, 4.26 in Limehouse. Diarrhœa 99 59 Measles 43 41 Diphtheria 34 29 Whooping Cough 36 24 Scarlet Fever 20 8 Fever (Typhoid, etc.) 9 8 Small-pox The mortality from diarrhoea, diphtheria, whooping cough and scarlet fever is seen from the foregoing table to be excessive in St. George's as compared with all London. The total absence of deaths from small-pox is in itself a valuable evidence as to the efficiency of modern preventive measures of vaccination, together with notification and prompt isolation. How long this immunity will continue under the new Vaccination Act remains to be seen. Diarrhœa. Amongst children diarrhoea is the most deadly preventable complaint in your parish. With the high figure of 99 it heads the table of mortality from zymotic diseases. Of this number 73 deaths occurred under the age of one year, 22 between one and five, and 4 at the age of five and upwards. In addition to the 99 deaths from diarrhoea coming under the general heading of "Specific Febrile or Zymotic Diseases "there were 36 deaths from enteritis placed under the class " Local Diseases." During the seven years 1892 to 1898 more deaths were due to diarrhoea than to any other of the seven principal zymotic diseases. This is seen in the following table :— TABLE VIII. Mortality in St. George's, from the seven principal Zymotic Diseases. Deaths. 1892 1893 1894 1895 1896 1897 1898 Total. Diarrhœa 53 63 72 61 64 111 99 523 Measles 66 22 100 71 85 67 43 454 Whooping Cough 46 59 64 56 55 32 34 346 Diphtheria ... 26 41 49 19 26 28 36 225 Scarlet Fever 15 41 15 14 10 14 20 114 Fever, chiefly Typhoid 8 12 5 9 7 11 9 61 Small pox 0 7 0 0 0 1 0 8 214 245 305 230 247 264 241 1731 The reason for the exceptional mortality from diarrhoea in 1898 was probably connected with the hot and dry weather of the third quarter of the year. This observation coincides with the climatic condition of 1897. 12 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. It has been proved that the temperature of the earth is one of the most important factors concerned in the development of the malady, and that the summer rise of diarrhœal mortality does not commence until the mean temperature recorded by the four-foot earth thermometer has attained somewhere about 56 degrees Fahrenhoit. From investigations made by Dr. Klein, it appears likely that summer diarrhoea is associated with the presence in the stools of a micro-organism, which he has named "Bacillus enteritidis sporogenes." This microbe presumably lives and multiplies in organically fouled and damp soil, and is given off in the air to contaminate and poison food, especially milk. The chief conditions of locality giving rise to diarrhoea in St. George's aro, I believe, the following:- (1) A loose damp soil polluted by liquid filth—e.g., from defective sowors. (2) Density of population. (3) Crowding of buildings upon areas. (4) Hindrances to the free circulation of air, both about and within dwellings. (5) Sewer emanations. (6) Accumulations of domestic refuse in ashpits and dustbins. (7) Absence of efficient control of dairies and of dairy farms supplying milk to local dairies. As regards tho conditions relating to the population, the chief causes predisposi ng to diarrhœa are:- (1) Poverty. (2) Mode of feeding of infants. (3) Maternal neglect and carelessness in infant management. (4) Unwholesome or improper place of storage for food and vessels connocted with food. The main suggestions to bo made are—To provide :— (1) Sound drainage system, both of houses and subsoil. (2) Impervious flooring to houses, so as to exclude ground air. (3) Free ventilation inside and outside houses. (4) Careful storage of food. (5) Systematic inspection of dairies, cow sheds, and dairy farms. (6) Boiling of milk, especially in summer. (7) Education of mothers as to the proper way of rearing infants. Measles. Measles is the mostdeadly complaint in your parish, and leads to a waste of life which, in my opinion, may bo traced to tho following causes (1) Measles, although highly infectious, is non-notifiable, and therefor# outside the control of your Sanitary Authority. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 13 (2) It is generally looked upon as a mild disorder that wants little or no treatment, whereas it requires careful isolation and nursing, as it is liable to many grave accidents. (3) Bad environment; measles does little harm among the well-to-do, but among the poor it is estimated that twenty to thirty die out of every hundred attacked by the disease. As frequently pointed out by me before, I think that the compulsory notification of cases of measles throughout London, with subsequent hospital treatment and disinfection, could not fail, in a poor parish like St. George's, to result in a great saving of life. Diphtheria Mortality. There has been a further increase in deaths from diphtheria. There were 19 deaths in 1895; 26 in 1896; 28 in 1897; and 36 in 1898. (These figures including membranous croup, according to the method of the Registrar-General.) There can be no doubt that the density of population, and the modern system of Board School education, have a considerable influence upon the prevalence of this highly infectious disease. As I have said in previous reports, it would be prudent. I think, to consider the desirability of the frequent and systematic inspection of all schools. It is in such places that early and undetected cases of diphtheria, in the sore throat stage, are to be found under circumstances that afford every opportunity for the spread of infection. Last year I quoted from a special Report made in 1890, pointing out the value and necessity of obtaining an early diagnosis in doubtful sore-throat. As the passage in question contains the pith of the matter, I venture again to insert it :—" Early Diagnosis : It is well known to medical men that some cases of diphtheria are extremely difficult to detect. The diagnosis can only be made after a period of waiting. Moreover, it seems probable that a certain number of cases are never detected at all. As an illustration of this difficulty I have it from a doctor that four of his patients in Cornbury Street, although suffering from suspicious sore-throats, remained unnotified owing to the clinical evidence being too weak, in his judgment, to support the theory of diphtheria. Hence it is of the utmost importance to be able to decide at once whether a sore-throat is or is not of a diphtheritic nature. This can now be done in a bacteriological laboratory. In Marylebone, for instance, every medical man is provided with a small cardboard box containing two sterilised test tubes. One of these tubes is for membrane if it can be obtained, and the other is furnished with a sterilised swab of wool wound round a wire. The medical attendant touches the throat with the wool and sends it off to the Medical Officer of Health, who is able to send back within twenty-four hours a definite report as to the existence of the disease. The cost of the whole examination of each case amounts to 2s. 7d. It should be clearly understood, however, that the examinations are not for the purpose of revising certificates, but for dividing the cases into those which might possibly be treated at home in safety, and those which require strict isolation in an infectious hospital." In March, 1898, in compliance with a resolution of your Vestry. I drew up a special Report upon the bacteriological diagnosis of doubtful cases of diphtheria and of typhoid fever. This communication will be found under Section III. of this Report. It deals fully and systematically with the whole matter of the scientific detection of the two diseases named in early and doubtful cases. 1 here is no doubt, in my own mind, that a public bacteriological service such as that suggested, would materially add to the powers of your Vestry in the control of infectious diseases, which is one of the most important duties of a Sanitary Authority. A free bacteriological examination has been established in at least eight metropolitan parishes, and their legal position has not been questioned by the Local Government Board. 14 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. Respiratory Diseases. TABLE IX. Sub-District. No. of Deaths—1897. Total. No. of Deaths—1898. Total. Bronchitis Pneumonia Phthisis Bronchitis Pneumonia 42 Phthisis. 70 Borough Road 47 41 67 155 47 159 London Road 39 34 42 115 41 34 70 145 Kent Road 71 48 63 182 58 48 52 158 Total 157 123 172 452 146 124 192 462 In 1892 the total mortality under this heading was 539, which fell to 419 in 1893, and to 363 in 1894, and has risen to 394 in 1895, to 372 in 1896, to 452 in 1897. and to 462 in 1898. These figures represent a rate above that of the whole of London. That excess may, I think, be somewhat closely connected with the dense acreage-population of the district, together with its largo proportion of tenement dwellers and the prevalence of indoor occupation. A good deal of the mortality among young children is due to lung troubles, which often follow measles and whooping cough. The returns of deaths from consumption are not so heavy as one would expect in so densely crowded and poverty-stricken a district. At the same time it should be noted that the registrars have to depend for these figures on the death certificates, and there can be no doubt that a certain number of cases are not correctly described. This observation applies especially to alcoholism, and to such hereditary and constitutional disorders as syphilis and consumption. Of late widespread attention has been drawn to the prevention of tubercular diseases, a subject of vast importance to the community. Roughly speaking, lung consumption accounts for one out of every eight deaths in the United Kingdom, an average that is exceeded in your parish. The disease results from the invasion of the human body by a minute bacillus, which may be breathed into the air passages in the form of dust or bo conveyed in the flesh or milk of infected animals. All that weakens an individual, makes him less able to resist the invasion of the tubercle bacillus, hence the value of a good environment. The prevention of consumption may be considered under the headings of:— (a) General hygiene. (b) The care of infected persons. (c) Supervision of meat and milk supplies. (a) General Hygiene.—This comes under the ordinary sanitary administration of your parish, but special stress should be laid upon the need of good ventilation and diainage, both of houses and workshops and upon the dangers of overcrowding. The great natural agencies in the destruction of the bacilli are fresh air and sunlight. (b) I he care of Infected Persons.— Every consumptive person is an active centre of infection, just as he would be if suffering from typhoid fever or small-pox. The most common way in which the dangerous bacilli are spread is by means of the expertorations. 1 The latter become dangerous when dried and blown about as dust. Persons suffering from the complaint, therefore, should always, both indoorn and out, a special spitcup, the contents of which should be burnt, or else he should spit into a paper handkerchief, which may be similarly disposed of. Under a rational system of treatment, isolation of phthisical patients is not necessary, but notification Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1698. 15 would nevertheless be a desirable step, as it would then be possible to make sure that a patient was not spreading the disease, and systematic disinfection of his surroundings could be undertaken. Disinfection after deaths from phthisis is now undertaken by your Vestry and urged upon parishioners. It would be advisable to distribute among your parishioners printed forms containing special information on this subject couched in plain and simple language. (c) Supervision of Meat and Milk Supply.—Although the subject of the transmission of phthisis from the lower animals to man is still more or less obscure, yet it seems hardly logical to attempt to stamp out the disease in man and yet allow him to go on consuming the flesh and milk of infected animals. The same bacillus causes tuberculosis in both instances. That it may be transmitted in an active or living state in the milk of an infected cow is known, and it is also an indisputable fact that in the United Kingdom many cows suffer from tuberculosis. Moreover, an undue proportion of infants, who are largely fed with raw milk, are affected with the disease. It would be well, therefore, in my opinion, for your Authority to consider carefully all measures that tend to the exclusion of the flesh or milk of tubercular animals from the food supply of the parishioners. flortality of Infants and Children. The death-rates of infants under one year, and of children under five years, afford valuable indirect evidence of the sanitary well-being of a community. During 1898 the number of infants who died within the first year of life in St. George's was 402. These figures give the high rate of 188 deaths to every 1,000 births in St. George's, as compared with 167 deaths per 1,000 births for London. The average rate for the years 1892 to 1898 for St. George's was 189, as against 162 for London. Of a total of 1,470 deaths at all ages in St. George's 667 occurred under five years, which gives the large percentage of 45 4 of the total number of deaths. It is well known to specialists in children's diseases that " wasting" constitutes one of the commonest causes of death among infants. Thousands of children die every year in London simply because they are fed on food which they cannot digest, and which acts as an irritant to the stomach and intestines. Mortality of Infants and Children. The death-rates of infants under one year, and of children under five years, afford valuable indirect evidence of the sanitary well-being of a community. During 1898 the number of infants who died within the first year of life in St. George's was 402. These figures give the high rate of 188 deaths to every 1,000 births in St. George's, as compared with 167 deaths per 1,000 births for London. The average rate for the years 1892 to 1898 for St. George's was 189, as against 162 for London. The following are the most prominent diseases causing the infant mortality under one vear of age:- TABLE X. Deaths 1894. 1895. 1896. 1897. 1898. Diarrhœa 54 41 35 84 73 Tabes Mesenterica (bowel phthisis) 10 38 27 51 30 56 22 28 13 30 Tubercular Meningitis, Hydrocephalus 12 10 14 4 14 Other Tubercular & Scrofulous Diseases 16 14 12 2 / 3 Premature Birth 44 39 28 31 29 Convulsions 63 69 42 29 37 Bronchitis 44 40 5 41 27 Pneumonia 16 38 38 30 30 Dentition 22 35 23 10 8 Whooping Cough 29 30 26 15 11 Suffocation in bed 20 16 15 13 29 Debility, Atrophy, and Inanition 88 69 65 45 32 All Other causes 8 24 44 74 96 Total 426 452 377 400 402 16 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southtwark. The many causes leading to loss of infant life in St. George's may be thus summarised:- (1) Bad environment, such us insanitary conditions, overcrowding, and absence of parks and open spaces. (2) Improper and insufficient food. (3) Mismanagement through maternal ignorance. (4) Early marriages. (5) Debility, disease, and dissipation of parents. (6) Maternal neglect, due to mothers being more or less employed away from home in factories and workshops. (7) Use of opiates; usually in the form of patent medicines. The question of how to lessen the excessive destruction of child-life is an important one. It is a complex result traceable to many causes, each of which demands careful consideration and separate treatment. Among general measures it may be broadly stated that anything which tends to raise the standard of wholesomeness of a district, will also help to diminish the infantile death-rate. Another valuable aid to prevention would be the education of parents generally in the simple laws of healthy living, with special reference to the diet and clothing of their offspring. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health.—1898. 19 SECTION II.—GENERAL. The Salvation Army Shelter. During the past year no cases of infectious disease have been reported from this ???helter. During the day time—that is to say, while devoid of inmates—the building as been visited by your Sanitary Inspectors. For many years past I have advocated ???le desirability of systematic night inspection of such resorts. Under present Conditions your Vestry have no legal right to make such visitation, which could ???adily be instituted, however, by including them within the Common Lodging ???ouses Act. The legal aspects have been established by proceedings taken by your ???uthority several years ago. Briefly stated, Her Majesty's Judges decided that these belters did not fall under the Common Lodging Houses Act, to which Mr. Justice ???Vills added: "The case was clearly one in which the Public Health Act, so far as isinfection was concerned, ought to apply, but does not." Public Lavatory Accommodation. During recent years much attention has been directed to the important subject of Public Lavatory accommodation. Thus two old-fashioned structures above-ground ???vere demolished at the time of the Jubilee; while, under the powers conferred by he Public Health (London) Act, 1891, your Authority constructed an underground Convenience, for men only, near the '• Elephant and Castle," in 1893. Plans approved by the Vestry at the time of my last Report awaited the result of Government inquiry, for the construction of two additional underground Conveni???nces, namely, one (for men only) at the Obelisk, St. George's Circus, and the other for both sexes) at the junction of the Old and New Kent Roads. Both have received Government approval, aud are now in course of construction. A third (for women only) is shortly to be established on the premises of the new Public Library, in the Borough Road. Of these two will be free for women and three for men. Before leaving this important subject it may be well to add, in the interests of the general public health, the following suggestions, which, if carried out, could hardly fail to add to the comfort and wellbeing of a poor and crowded population :— (a) The provision at each of these points of accommodation for both sexes. (b) Ample free accommodation generally for both sexes, which appears to be imperatively called for in a poor district. Alcohol. The question of alcohol has an important bearing upon the wellbeing of any community, but more especially in dealing with a poor and crowded district like that of St. George the Martyr. Nowadays, the fact is widely recognised that mortality statistics furnish little trustworthy evidence as to the actual injury to life that results from the abuse of alcohol. Thus, very many deaths attributed to diseases of organs, such as the kidneys or the liver, are in reality due to intemperance. Another great contributing factor is that the drinking habit, even in its milder phases, renders its victims much less able to resist the onset of acute maladies. There can be no doubt that alcoholism is in many cases to be partly attributed—in its first stages, at any 20 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. rate—to the desire for stimulation of energies that have been exhausted by overwork, hunger, exposure to insanitary conditions, and other evils incidental to a poor environment. The whole question strikes deep into the social life of the community, and deserves careful attention in its many aspects by the administrators of sanitary laws, whether local or central. Complaint of Nuisance arising from Burial Vaults beneath the Parish Church. On the 17th March, 1898, a complaint was made to me by the Rector of the Parish Church, the Rev. W. J. Sommerville, as to an alleged nuisauce arisiug from bodies buried in the crypt. He stated that some of his parishioners had complained of bad smells entering the Church, and asked me to inspect the building. On the 24th March I visited the Church, and as a result of that visit I reported the matter to the Public Health Committee on the 28th. On April 25th I made an inspection of the crypt, with Mr. Ralph, late District Inspector. On the evening of the 25th I reported to the Public Health Committee that I had fouud in the crypt upwards of one thousand coffins, that the air of the crypt was musty, that there were cracks in masonry of roof and walls, and that in my opinion the state of affairs was such as to be likely to be a danger to health. On May 3rd, in answer to a question addressed to me in the Vestry, I stated my general conclusion as to the insanitary state of the crypt. I also stated that " the first and only inspection of the crypt made by me was on the 25th April, 1898, on the complaint of Mr. Sommerville, and that no other official complaint of a nuisance or danger to health from the crypt had ever been made to me." On May 9th the matter having been referred back to the Health Committee, with power to act, at the request of the Chairman for a report, I referred to my previous verbal report on the 25th April, and to the written record in my official diary. I recommended the Committee to communicate with the Home Office, which alone had powers to deal with nuisances arising from burials. A few days later I received a communication from Dr. Hoffman asking me to accompany him on a Home Office inspection of the crypt, to take place on the 19th May. Together with the Chairman of the Health Committee, the Rector, the Churchwardens, and Mr. Hilton, I was present at the official inspection on the date mentioned. So far as I was concerned as Medical Officer of Health, that ended the matter for the official year under report, namely, 1898. Up to the 31st December, no steps had been taken, to abate the state of affairs complained of by Mr. Sommerville to your Sanitary Authority, and pronounced a danger to health by the Home Office. Hortuary and Coroner's Court. 204 bodies were removed to the Parish Mortuary during the year 1898, as against I 179 in 1897 and 212 in 1896. This number includes bodies brought from the St. George's Workhouse. Inquests were held in 122 cases with 96 post mortem examinations. During 1898, 29 young children were suffocated whilst lying in bed with their parents, as against 13 in 1897. This lamentable loss of life might have been prevented had these unfortunate children slept in cots. The Factory and Workshop Act, 1891. 369 Workshops have been registered by your Authority since January 1892, when the Act first came into operation. Of this number 31 have been added during the past year. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 21 The Order remains only partly enforced in this parish, and will continue so unless some means be devised to lighten the more pressing duties of the Sanitary Inspectors. In my Annual Reports from 1893 to 1897, I stated my opinion that a special inspector would be required to carry the Order out thoroughly in your district. This view of the question I would again respectfully urge upon the attention of your Vestry, especially considering the added duties imposed by the 1895 Act of Mr. Asquith. The Factory and Workshop Act, 1895. The chief points may be briefly touched upon. First and foremost the Act provides that a factory or workshop shall be deemed overcrowded if there be less than 250 feet of cubic space to each person during the hours of work, and of 400 cubic feet during overtime. It authorises a Magistrate, on complaint of an inspector, and being satisfied that a factory or workshop is in a dangerous or injurious condition, to prohibit the place from being used until such works as are necessary to remove the danger have been executed. Adequate penalties are provided against the employment of persons in work injurious to health, or for allowing wearing apparel to be made up, cleaned, or repaired in places where there is scarlet fever or small-pox. It also directs that a full notification and register of deaths from accident be kept by owners or occupiers, and that the Factory Inspector attend the subsequent inquest, while the Home Secretary is furnished with powers for additional investigation if he deem fit. Numerous provisions have been made with a view to enforcing the duties of employers in the observance of sanitation, of the fencing of machines, of the affixing of notices, and of the general carrying out of their responsibilities under the Act. It is worthy of special note, that in the case of tenement factories the owner is made responsible in place of the occupier. Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1800. In my last Annual Report I suggested that two ''unhealthy" areas should be dealt with in Ward 1. These areas have since been included in a larger scheme set forth in a Special Report presented to your Vestry on the 13th December (see page 32) The whole cost of carrying out of any such scheme would devolve upon the London County Council as Local Authority. In view of the overcrowded and insanitary condition of the areas in question I would urge your Vestry to lay the matter forthwith before the Council. Houses and Tenements 3 Inhabitants 17 From the year 1892 to 1898, applications have been made for Closing Orders in the case of 322 houses and tenements unfit for human habitation. These dwellings have sheltered upwards of 1,517 persons, and I think some steps should be taken to re-house those whose occupations necessitate their living in or near the district.
