As in previous years, I would again respectfully draw the attention of your Sanitary Authority to this pressing problem. I have also advised demolition of Nos. 1, 2, 3, 4 and 5, Red Cow Alley, of 25. Barron's Place, and of 6a & 9, Emmett's Cab Yard. In the case of the houses in Red Cow Alley action has been taken by the Vestry. Bakehouses under the New (1895) Act. The general provisions of the Act enforces 400 cubic feet of air space for each workman engaged in a retail bakehouse. They also appear to allow the Local Authorities to proceed against the owner as well as the occupier, for certain offences committed in any such bakehouses. The special requirements of the Act as regards retail bakehouse are as follows:— "Section 27.—(1) Sections 34 and 35 of the principal Act shall apply to every bakehouse, and so much of those Sections as limits the operation thereof to cities, towns, and places having a population of more than five thousand persons, shall be repealed. "(2) In Section 15 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1883, the words "which was not so let or occupied before the first day of June, 1883,' shall be repealed. "(3) A place underground shall not be used as a bakehouse, unless it is so used at the commencement of this Act, and if any place is so used in contravention of this Act it shall be deemed to be a workshop not kept in conformity with the principal Act." It will be seen, after thirty years of tentative legislation, Government has recognised the fact that underground bakehouses should cease to exist. The prohibition for any underground workplace used afresh as a bakery after the 1st January, 1896, may bo reasonably regarded as a first step towards their entire removal. The Notification of Infectious Diseases. Certain maladies have been made notifiable in London since the passing of the Infectious Diseases (Notification) Act, 1889. They comprise:—Small-pox, cholera, diphtheria, membranous croup, erysipelas, the disease known as scarlatina, or scarlet fever, and the fevers known by any of the following names: typhus, typhoid, enteric, relapsing, continued or puerperal. In 1898 the sum in fees paid to medical practitioners for notifying 681 infectious cases in St. George's amounted to £70 6s. 6d. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 23 This amount, paid in the first instance by your Vestry, is recouped by the Asylums Board, and charged to the Metropolitan Common Poor Fund. 75.6 per cent. of the total number of cases notified in the parish during 1898 were removed to the Asylums Board and other hospitals. It is important to bear in mind that prompt isolation alone can obviate the danger resulting from the detention of infectious patients in so poor and densely packed a district as St. George's. Typhus Fever. On the 12th April, a case of typhus fever was notified to me, in the parish, and at once removed to the South Eastern Hospital. The patient was a woman who had been exposed to the disease in Kensington, where she had attended a family in which typhus fever had recently existed. The case was thus an imported one, and no further case arising from it was notified in the parish—a fact which may be fairly attributed to prompt removal of exposed persons to the reception house, and to thorough subsequent disinfection. In Kensington, on the contrary, where there is no reception house, there were many cases and several deaths. Another case of typhus fever was notified on the 22nd October, six months later. It was that of a young man who was found to have the disease upon presenting himself as an out-patient at Guy's Hospital. The source of infection could not be ascertained, but up to the end of the year no further attack was notified. Full details of this and of the preceding case will be found in the Special Reports (pages 27 and 30). Room Disinfection. It will be within the memory of your Vestry, that, during the year 1896, a considerable amount of attention was given to the subject of room disinfection. As a result of my personal experience and investigation I expressed the conclusion that the system of administration as then practised was imperfect. Among the chief causes of such imperfection the following were mentioned (page 24 of my Annual Report for 1896):— (a) Want of a refuge house. (b) Want of a larger staff of disinfectors. (c) Want of a special inspector to deal with infectious diseases. (d) Want of promptitude of the Asylums Board in the removal of cases. With regard to the foregoing, it may be stated that a Refuge House has been established and is in working order; that in 1896 an addition was made in the staff of disinfectors in the person of a Superintending Disinfector who, with his wife, lives in the Reception House ; and that the question of the special inspector is still in abeyance. Since I made the suggestion for the appointment of the latter in my Annual Report for 1896, the duties of the District Inspectors have been considerably increased with regard to the investigation and supervision of infectious cases. In this way their work is to some extent curtailed in other directions, and the desirability of appointing a special Fever Inspector is thereby emphasised. Lastly, considerable improvement has taken place in the adequacy of the hospital accommodation provided by the Metropolitan Asylums Board. Reception House. This link in the preventive handling of infectious diseases is no less necessary than the allied measures of notification, isolation and disinfection. In a poor and 24 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. crowded parish it is imperative to provide a temporary shelter for persons who have been exposed to infection and in whose homes disinfection is being carried out. This principle has been recognised by your Vestry in the foundation of a Reception House at considerable outlay. During the year 1898, a total of 149 persons were received ; of that number 66 were adults and 83 children. Considering the difficulty of inducing parishioners to take advantage of a novel departure of this kind the Vestry may be congratulated on the foregoing figures, which will in all probability be largely iucroased in the near future. Mouse to House Inspection. The first section of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, directs that a house to house inspection shall be made by the Local Authority. Such a visitation as that intended by the framers of the Act, was for the first time in the history of this Parish, commenced in 1894. During 1898 such inspections have been made of 1455 houses as against 1300 in 1897 and 1665 in 1890, and the results of such visits have been entered into the registers kept for the purpose. To repeat the words made use of lor the last four years, I may perhaps bo allowed to add my opinion that this system of house to house inspection is likely to do more towards raising the general health standard of your district than any single step yet taken by your Vestry. Houses Let in Lodgings. So far, 421 dwelling houses, inhabited by the poorest classes, have been registered in conformity with the Bye-laws. In this parish, houses built on the so-called "model" or block system, and accommodating upwards of 12,800 inmates, at present escape the salutary provisions of the new bye-laws. These dwellings, often inhabited by the uneducated and unruly poor, are for the most part badly supervised. I would beg once more to suggest that such "models" should bo included, in the interests of the public health, within the purview of the new tenement bye-laws. Such a reform could be accomplished by means of an amendment of the Public Health Act. During the past year Miss Elliott has again done good work as regards cleansing and tho abating of overcrowding, as will be seen by reference to her report. Falcon Court and Neighbourhood. Last year I reported that there appeared to be every likelihood that the unwholesome area of Falcon Court and its neighbourhood would be swept away at an early date and replaced by an open air-space. A Local Government inquiry held in 1896 insisted upon the necessity of housing the population that would be displaced under the scheme. Accordingly the London County Council prepared plans for the erection of artizans' dwellings in the Borough Road. These plans were submitted to the Local Government Board and passed. Representations were made by your Vestry to the London County Council pointing out the urgency of the case and the continued delay in carrying out the proposed scheme. I am now able to report that the foundations of the new buildings have been laid in the Borough Road. It is to be hoped that before the lapse of another year this important clearance of an unhealthy area will have been accomplished. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898 25 SECTION III.—SPECIAL REPORTS. Special Report upon the Bacteriological Diagnosis of doubtful cases of Diphtheria and of Typhoid Fever. In compliance with a resolution adopted by your Vestry on the 8th ultimo, I herewith beg to submit the following Special Report. The directions contained in your resolution relate to the two diseases, diphtheria and typhoid or enteric fever. Clearly, the tests referred to may be applied before, during, and after an attack, and have a special value in each of those stages. Before the Attack.—In using the term "before," I mean it to apply to those cases in which the disease, though present, cannot be detected, with certainty, by any means yet known to medical science. This fore-running or doubtful stage occurs in diphtheria and typhoid fever more often than in any other malady with which we are called upon as sanitary administrators to deal. In handling diseases of so highly infectious a nature it is evident that there is an especial danger attached to non-detection at the onset of an attack. In the case of diphtheria, it is just as contagious during the early days as at any other period. Hence, any trustworthy means of diagnosis is of the utmost value, not only to the patient, but also to others with whom he may be brought into contact. A similar remark does not apply with equal force to typhoid fever, but, nevertheless, it is an important point to be able to say with certainty about the end of the first week of the illness that an attack is or is not typhoid fever, the more so as it occasionally happens that the medical attendant may not be able to decide the point even after the lapse of a fortnight. During the Attack.—Here the tests are only of value in determining the nature of doubtful cases. It may be at once said that the chief value of both these bacteriological methods of diagnosis applies chiefly to ill-defined attacks. No one who is skilled in practical medicine could fail to recognize at a glance a well-marked case either of diphtheria or of typhoid fever. The most experienced man in the world, however, will be undecided for some time in a certain proportion of attacks of both these maladies. The bacteriological method would therefore be of value in settling differences of opinion between medical men, and in the case of typhoid fever would at once determine the proper and only safe line of treatment. After the Attack.—In diphtheria the absence of the special microbe that causes the disease marks the stoppage of the infectivity of the patient. This can now bo ascertained by exact bacteriological examination. As regards diphtheria the question is of particular importance, as a patient in a town environment is far more likely to be a centre of disease when recovering from diphtheria than from typhoid. METHODS OF TESTING. In the case of diphtheria both secretion and membrane from the patches that form in the throat are collected and sent to the laboratory. The throat of the patient is touched with a little swab of wool, which is replaced in a sterilised tube, and sent to the Medical Offier of Health by post in an appropriate package. In the case of typhoid fever a drop or two of blood is drawn off from a prick in the patient's finger by means of a small sterilised glass tube, the euds of which are 6ealed, and the whole forwarded to the laboratory. 26 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. METHODS OF COLLECTING SPECIMENS. The plan usually followed is to provide every medical man in the district with outfits suited for both the foregoing purposes. COST. The total cost of the laboratory examination varies from 2s. 6d. to 3s. 6d., the latter price being authorised at St. Pancras. To the above amounts must be added the original cost of outfit and the postage to and from the Health Department. It should be borne in mind, however, in considering the question of cost, that the method is needed only in doubtful cases. HOW FAR IS THE BACTERIOLOGICAL METHOD PRACTISED? St. Pancras and St. Olave's adopt the plan both for diphtheria and for typhoid fever, and, so far as can be ascertained, are the only Sanitary Authorities in London which follow that course. For diphtheria alone the public test is applied in St. Marylebone, Holborn and Strand, while Camberwell has made arrangements to do the same shortly at a cost of four shillings per case. Poplar has a similar course under serious consideration. From Mr. Murphy's London County Council Report for 1896, wo learn that in St. Marylebono, material from 87 cases was examined with positive results in 46; in Holborn 17 with the like result in 10. This gives a decision in the affirmative of over fifty per cent. in 104 presumably doubtful cases to which the test was applied. It should be noted that the bacteriological method of diagnosing diphthoria has been carried out for some years past in many provincial districts, notably in Bristol, Brighton, Manchester, and Liverpool. In some of them tho typhoid fever test has also been instituted. The pioneers in this extremely practical departure in public health work hail from America. My own conclusions upon this important subject may be briefly indicated as follows:— 1. That it is of great value from a practical standpoint to detect diphthoria and typhoid fever with certainty, especially in the early and late stages. In both cases tho method may be now and then essential in the prevention of the spread of the disease. 2. That the benefit will be both individual and general: (1) Individual, in tho determination of doubtful cases, and of the proper course of treatment, and in saving him from unnecessary exposure. (2) General (a), in the detection of cases, and ho of indicating the need of isolation and disinfection; (6) in preventing uncured cases in children from returning to school, and there becoming fresh centres of infection. 3. That the possibility of such detection is now rendered scientifically cortain by means of a bacteriological examination of the secretions of diphtheria, and of the blood of patients suffering from typhoid fever. 4. That the actual cost to the Vestry would be small in comparison with tho gain to patients and the prevention of the spread of disease. 5. That, so far as I have been able to learn, the exporienco of those Sanitary Authorities who have adopted the tests has been satisfactory, although perhaps medical men have not availed themselves of the proffered opportunities so generally as had been anticipated. That, in my opinion, the adoption of both tests would be of distinct value to the sanitary administration of tho District of St. George the Martyr. 8th March, 1898. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health.—1898. 27 Special Report on a case of Typhus Fever notified in April, 1898. I am happy to be able to report that the case of Mrs. E., of 20, Toulmin Street, notified and removed to hospital with typhus fever on the 12th April last, has not been followed by any spread of this extremely contagious and fatal disease. The wisdom of Parliament in casting the duty of enforcing S. 60—(4) of the Public Health (Lond.) Act, upon those responsible for the safeguarding of the health of the people is, I think, sufficiently apparent in this case. The section referred to, as your Vestry will remember, reads as follows:— "The Sanitary Authority shall provide, free of charge, temporary shelter or house accommodation, with any necessary attendants for the members of any family in which any dangerous infectious disease has appeared, who have been compelled to leave their dwellings for the purpose of enabling such dwellings to be disinfected by the Sanitary Authority." On the question of the usefulness of Eeception Houses in stamping out diseases such as typhus, I cannot, perhaps, do better than quote the following passage from the "Medical Press," which puts the matter—so to speak—in a nutshell:— "Last Easter a case of typhus was reported in St. George's, Southwark. The patient, a woman, contracted the disease from nursing the children of a family that had been attacked in Kensington. The family she visited consisted of a man, wife, and seven children, who all took the fever, together with the doctor and undertaker. In another flat on the same floor lived three persons —father, mother, and child— who were infected by a child of the former family sleeping there after the death of the mother. Kensington, wealthy parish though it be, has no reception house for the isolation of persons who have been exposed to infection. In striking contrast comes tbe case of St. George's, Southwark, which has recently provided itself with a substantial and well-appointed shelter of the kind. The parishioner was promptly removed to the South Eastern Fever Hospital, and her husband quarantined for fourteen days, while the premises were thoroughly cleansed. In the same house, living in five small rooms, were four families, who were at once removed to the reception house for two days and a night. Thanks to this radical and clear-sighted policy, there can be little doubt that the poverty-stricken and densely-crowded parish of St. George's has been saved from the disaster of an epidemic of typhus, whereas unwise Kensington suffered to the extent of fourteen cases and three deaths, including the medical attendant. The Southwark Vestry may be congratulated on the brilliant success of their experiment." To this I might add. that the Medical Officer of Health of Kensington,. in a copy of a report he has been good enough to forward me, says he is of opinion that Mrs. E., of 20, Toulmin Street, Southwark, "probably would have escaped taking typhus, had it been possible to place her in a shelter whilst the rooms of the two flats at Western Dwellings, Kensington, were being disinfected." 24th May, 1898. The need of further free Public Lavatory Accommodation for Women and Children. The following communication and copy of letter have been received by me:— London County Council, Public Health Department, Spring Gardens, S.W., 8th July, 1898. Re Public Conveniences for Women and Children in London. Dear Sir,— I am directed by a Committee of the Council to forward you for your information the enclosed copy of a letter received by the Council from Mr, Gerald C. Parsons, 28 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. of No. 50. Wray Crescent, Tollington Park, N., calling attention to the alleged neglect to supply adequate water-closet accommodation in London for women and children. I am, Dear Sir, Yours faithfully SHIRLEY F. MURPHY. Medical Officer of Health Dr. F. Waldo, Vestry Hall, 81, Borough Road, S.E. [Copy.] 50, Wray Crescent, Tollinoton Park, N., 10th June, 1898. Sir,— I venture to suggest that the attention of the different Vestries of London should he drawn to the danger to public health and morality owing to the neglect to supply in certain crowded districts proper public accommodation for women and children (partly free). Amongst such districts might be named the Elephant and Castle, the Britannia, Hampstead Road, St. Paul's Churchyard, the Angel, Islington, Ac. London being such an immense forest of houses, these places must be situated centrally to be of any use to strangers to a locality, and surely it is to the interest of Londoners to encourage tourists to visit the metropolis, though this is viewing it from the lowest point of view. I would further suggest that it is desirable that all conveniences for women should be labelled "for women and children." I am, Sir, Your obedient servant, (Signed) GERALD C. PARSONS. In reference to this important matter, I may perhaps bo permitted to state for the information of new members of the Vestry, that your attention was called to the further need of the provision of accommodation for women in my Annual Reports for 1895 and 1896. In these reports I suggested "as a first step in the right direction a women's lavatory might be added to the one existing near the "Elephant and Castle." Again, referring to the same subject in my annual report for 1817, I said that "During the year under report much attention has been displayed to the important subject of public lavatory accommodation. "Two old-fashioned structures above ground were demolished at the time of the Jubilee. Under the powers conferred by the public Health (London) Act, 1891, your Authority constructed an underground convenience for men only near the 'Elephant and Castle' in the year 1893. "Plans have been approved by the Vestry and await the result of Government inquiry, for the construction of two additional underground conveniences, namely, one (for men only) at the Obelisk, St. George's Circus, and the other (for both sexes) at the junction of the Old and New Kent Roads. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 29 "Before leaving this important subject it may be well to add in the interests of the general public health the following suggestions, which if carried out could hardly fail to add to the comfort and well-being of a poor and crowded population:—(a) The provision at each of these points of accommodation for both sexes; (b) Ample free accommodation for both sexes, which appears to be imperatively called for in a poor district." At the present time only one public convenience exists for women, namely, that situated in New Alley near the Parish Church. Tenders have, however, been received for the construction of conveniences at (a) the junction of the Old and New Kent Roads, and (b) the rear of the new Public Library Buildings in the Borough Road. On the completion of these works I submit that three w.c's. to be provided free for the use of women will prove to be quite inadequate for the needs of the densely populated parish of St. George's, Southwark, and more especially so when one takes into consideration the constant and well-founded complaints of nuisances due to the improper use by strangers of conveniences in block buildings and of blind alleys and entries. 26th July, 1898. Report Re Suggested Demolition of No. 24, Barron's Place. I should advise your Vestry—the "Local Authority"—to take action under Sec. 36 of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, with a view to the demolition of No. 24, Barron's Place, Waterloo road. This dwelling-house was closed in February, 1895, on a Magistrate's order under the provisions of the Housing Act, and is in my opinion in such a condition as to be "dangerous or injurious to the health of the public or of the inhabitants of the neighbouring dwelling-houses." 20th September, 1898. (The advice given in this Report has not yet been acted upon.) Special Report on the Housing of the Poor. Section 30 of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, states that "It shall be the duty of the Medical Officer of Health of every district to represent to the local Authority of that district any dwelling-house which appears to him to be in a state so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation. The machinery of the Act also provides that such representation shall originate in one of two ways: either— 1. Indirectly on the written complaint to the Medical Officer of Health of any four or more householders living in or near a street in which any dwelling-house is thought to be in a state unfit for human habitation ; or 2. On the direct representation of the Medical Officer of Health to the "Local Authority"—that is to say, in this District, to your Vestry itself. In compliance with this statutory duty, I beg to report that Nos. 187,189, and 161, Waterloo Road, are in such a state so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation; and would accordingly advise your Vestry to puss the usua1 resolution to the effect "That notices be served on the owner or owners to make the premises fit for human habitation within a specified time, and, failing which, that proceedings be taken before a Court of summary jurisdiction for prohibiting the use of the same," 30 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. I would also advise your Vestry to pass a resolution in accordance with Section 33 of the Housing Act, to the effect "That it is expedient to order the demolition of the buildings," namely, Nos. 1, 2, 3,4, and 5, Red Cow Alley, Tabard Street. These dwelling-houses were represented by me to your Vestry as being unfit for human habitation, on the 96th February, 1895, and were closed by means of a Magistrate's order on the 24th March, 1895. Nothing has been done to these buildings—which still remain closed—and their continuance in that state is, in my opinion to quote the words of the Act—"dangerous or injurious to the health of the public, or of the inhabitants of the neighbouring dwelling-houses. 4th October, 1898. Special Report on a recent case of Typhus Fever in the Parish of St. George the Martyr, with General and Special Remarks thereon. Mr. Chairman,— In the statistical report which I present to you to-night, mention is made of a case of typhus in your district. An occurrence of that kind raises issues of so much importance that I have thought it best to embody my remarks thereon in the form of a special Report. On the 22nd October, I received a notification of a case of typhus fever at Guy's Hospital. The patient was a man, F.K., aged nineteen, who came from 2, Walker Street, Tabard Street. Alter about a week's illness at home, he went to the hospital, where he was detained as an in-patient, and his case notified as typhus on the 19th of October. By error this notification was sent to Bermondsey in the first place, so that it did not reach me before the morning of the 22nd October. I immediately wont to the patient's house and inquired into the circumstances of the case, and without delay urged upon the mother the necessity of disinfection and cleansing, to which she agreed, together with the removal of herself and children to the Reception House. The father went away to stay in the house of a son living in Bermondsey, but two days later I induced him also to come to the Reception House, and meanwhile communicated to the Medical Officer of Health of Bermondsey the fact that a person who had been exposed to the infection of typhus has entered his parish. The patient was a brushmaker by occupation, and had been at work up to the time of his illness. The circumstances of the family were fairly easy for the labouring class. The father was a casual river-side labourer ; three boys were earning wages, and a girl attended school. The family living in the house numbered man and wife, three sons, aged 19 (the patient), 10, 14, and a girl upwards of 10. The house contained two rooms, a living one on the ground floor (with a capacity of 132 cubic feet and floor space of 22 square feet per person) and a bed room upstairs reached from the lower room by stairs. The whole family of six adults (counting as an adult everyone over 10 as laid down in the bye-laws of this parish for lodging. houses) slept in three beds in the upstairs room. The cubic space of the bed room (making no deduction for beds or other contents) was 1,095 cubic feet, leaving 182 5 cubic feet per head, whereas tho minimum allowance under tho above quoted byelaws is 300 cubic feet for each inmate of a bed room, used exclusively as a sleeping apartment. I may add, that, in my opinion, the allowance per head should bo at least l,000 cubic feet, and that, only in the presence of free ventilation. In this particular bed room under notice there were two windows, an open fireplace and a doorway. The windows were on one side of the room, opposite a sloping roof on the other side so that there was no opportunity of cross ventilation. Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. 31 Turning next to the floor space, we find it a few inches over 25 square feet per inmate, which is, in my opinion, inadequate. The general condition of the house was fairly clean. The w.c. was at back, in a small yard closely hemmed in by houses. Walker Street is composed of small two and four-roomed houses, and lies in the midst of a network of streets and courts of similiar small dwellings, closely packed together. The inhahitant3 of this area are chiefly fish curers, hawkers, costermongers and labourers. The source of the infection in F.K., I have been unable to trace. Since the above case, another patient has been notified to me as suffering from typhus. The patient was removed to the South-Eastern Fever Hospital on the 30th October. His symptoms were obscure and misleading, but subsequently a postmortem examination revealed the fact that death was not due to typhus. Previously to the case of F.K., the only typhus notified to me in this parish occurred in April, 1898. Infection was traced to a patient at Kensington. Before that, no case had been reported since my appointment as Medical Officer in 1892; but a serious outbreak of the malady took place in 1864 and 1865. GENERAL OBSERVATIONS. Typhus, it is well known, is a most infectious disease, with a period of " sickening " of seven to fourteen days, and has not yet been traced to a definite micro-organism. It is quite certain that the chief cause of its spread are overcrowding and depressing conditions, such as poverty, want of food, intemperance, bad ventilation and insanitary environment. It is a disease of the poor, spread mainly by personal contact, and attacks chiefly children and young adults. In patients over middle age it is exceedingly fatal. The importance of any one case as regards the rest of London may perhaps best be shown by means of a particular example. The case spoken of as occurring in this parish early in that year was traced to North Kensington. Your parishioner who was attacked, went to nurse some sick relatives in the suburb mentioned, where she contracted the disease. The Kensington outbreak involved about fourteen cases, with three deaths (including that of the doctor in attendance). To quote the words of the Medical Officer of Health—Dr. Dudfield —our parishioner " probably would have escaped taking typhus, had it been possible to place her in a shelter whilst the rooms of the two flats at W.D. . . . were being disinfected." This incident shows how necessary it is for the welfare of the Metropolis for all districts to be equally well provided with sanitary safeguards, and also how the occurrence of an infectious disease in one quarter is a possible danger to the inhabitants of any or all other quarters. THE SPECIAL PRECAUTIONS TAKEN. These consisted in disinfection of the surroundings of the patient. The bedding and clothes were burnt, and the house disinfected and cleansed at the cost of the authority. The other inmates of the house were taken to the Reception House, and retained over the full incubation period of the disease, fourteen days. At the same time they were personally cleansed and their clothes disinfected. CONCLUSIONS. From a careful consideration of the foregoing facts, I conclude that this case, if not actually contracted from disease germs latent in the house, wa* certainly 32 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. rendered more dangerous by the crowded condition as to space both of the house and the neighbourhood. It is possible, but in my opinion not in the least probable, that the malady may have been conveyed in the materials from foreign source used in the patient's employment. If the Vestry think that any further action should be taken with regard to this congested district, the existence of which is specially dangerous in the presence of infectious disease, I would point out as a first step the necessity of providing better housing accommodation for the poor of this parish. In my opinion, powers are already in existence under Part III. of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, whereby the London County Council as Local Authority under the Act are empowered to provide dwellings for the working classes. The intention of this act appears to be the relief of overcrowding in poor districts, and it seems clear that it could hardly anywhere be applied for that purpose more appropriately than in the parish of St. George's, Southwark. 15th November, 1898. Special Report on an unhealthy area and vacant sites as the basis of an official representation to the London County Council with a view to the better housing of the poor of the Parish of St. George the Martyr, in accordance with the Council's resolutions of 29th November, 1898. Mr. Chairman, Your attention has been recently drawn to a local congestion of population in your parish by the occurrence of a case of typhus in a house the conditions of which showed what there need be little hesitation in describing as gross overcrowding. The subject is by no means new for it has been before the parish in a more or less pointed form for some years past. Recent decisions of the London County Council have brought the whole matter into such prominence that it can hardly be neglected by the London parishes, much less by those which are inhabited mainly by a working population. It may be well to preface my remarks with a short statement as to overcrowding, which may be considered under the headings of (1) Space, and (2) Housing. (1) With regard to space, which term is here confined to ground space the density of population in the whole of London is 58 per acre, whereas in its several parishes St. George the Martyr comes second on the list with 212 per acre, and follows Whitechapel with 220 per acre. It is only fair, however, to state that in my opinion the acreage density of population must be accepted with some reserve as an ultimate test of over-crowding, because much depends on the class of dwellings existing on any particular site, especially as regards the size and proper ventilation of rooms, and the sanitary equipment and maintenance of the buildings generally. (2) W ith regard to house overcrowding, the following facts are drawn from cases that have been dealt with by your parish during the eleven months ending 30th November, 1898. (a) Specific cases of overcrowding reported by district inspectors and abated. Ward I. During the 11 months 23 Ward II. „ „ 17 Ward III, „ „ „ „ 14 54 Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898 33 (b) Specific cases reported by Inspector for Houses let in lodgings during the above mentioned period 299. The total abatement from both sources therefore equals 353. That statement lone suffices to prove the existence of serious overcrowding in your parish. That it onsiderably understates the case will be abundantly evident when we re realise that be Special Inspector for Lodging Houses has not rigorously adopted the standard aid down by the Bye-laws which demand 300 cubic feet per inmate for bedroom sed for sleeping purposes only, and 400 cubic feet per head when not so occupied xclusively. Under the less stringent conditions then, your Authority has abated vercrowding in 299 houses during the past eleven months: or under all circumtances in a grand total of 353 houses. The recent resolutions of the London County Council upon the question of over;rowding run thus:— (a) That housing accommodation should be provided for a number of persons equal to that of the working classes displaced by any scheme under the Housing of he Working Classes Act, 1890, or under the provision of any Improvement Act; but not necessarily in the immediate neighbourhood of the displacement, due consideration being given to the needs of those living on any particular area. (b) That all clearances under the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, which involve re-housing be done at the sole cost of the Council. It will be evident that the above resolutions are based upon the principle of an. equal distribution of the responsibilities of the metropolis in sanitary mattersf Henceforth poorer parishes will be able to approach the problem of the housing o, bhe poor without the necessity of imposing fresh burdens upon already heavily rated and poor districts. Under these circumstances it is tolerably certain that many of the poorer parishes will hasten to avail themselves of the advantages thus offered them by the London County Council. In your own parish the need of immediate action is emphasised by the facts that your district is one of the most heavily rated, one of the poorest, and one of the most densely populated in the Metropolis, while the death-rate has always been abnormally high. I would, therefore, urge the Vestry to take immediate steps to secure the advantages thrown open to them by the new policy of the County Council. To reduce the position of your Vestry, as far as possible, to a short and practical form I would suggest that you approach the Council with definite proposals as to (1) unhealthy area for clearance and reconstruction, and (2) certain sites, now vacant, to be used for rehousing. The desirability of providing fresh sites for houses results from the fact of the Council's decision that dwelling accommodation has to be provided on the improvement area for the same number of persons of the working class as is displaced. The unhealthy area which I suggest should be dealt with by the Council lies in Ward I. (St. Michael's) on the north of the parish. It may be conveniently divided into three sections, referred to hereafter as the Grotto Place, the King's Bench Walk, and the Webber Bow unhealthy area. 1. The Grotto Place unhealthy area has been described as a triangular portion bounded on the north by Suffolk Grove and the Couuty Council Fire Brigade Station, with the two remaining sides of the triangle running southwards, and made up of Southwark Bridge Road and Lant Place. This small area I would extend to a larger triangle, bounded roughly on the north by Suffolk Grove, in St. Saviour s, and on the two remaining sides stretching southwards by Great Suffolk Street and Sturge Street. The smaller triangular portion of this area comprises a dense network of narrow courts and blind alleys. 34 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. 2. The King's Bench Walk area is bounded roughly by Pocock Street on the north, and by Friar Street on the south. The remaining two sides of a quadrilateral area are formed by King's Bench Walk and Wellington Place, running in a S.E. direction, both sides of which streets being included. In addition to this roughly quadrilateral area, is an outstanding portion at its S.E. corner, comprising Upper Green Street and Bean Street (both sides) and bounded on the south by King James Street. Upon this King's Bench Walk area certain sites would be left untouched.: They are the Pocock Street Board School, caretaker's house, and playground; the L.C.C. dwelling-houses, and a lamp factory recently built. The Webber Row unhealthy area is bounded on the north by Webber Street, and stretches down in a south-west direction as a rough quadrilateral, bounded on the western side by Webber Row, on the eastern by Peabody Square Buildings, and on the southerly extremity by Waterloo Road. The acreage and population covered by these three sections is approximately as as follows:— 1. The Grotto Place unhealthy area - (a) Smaller section, half-an-acre, with a population of 250. (b) The large section (inclusive of preceding) three acres, with a population of 856, # 2. The King's Bench Walk area, two-and-a-quarter acres, with a population of 879. 3. The Webber Row area, two acres, with a population of 293. It will thus be seen that the total acreage of the unhealthy areas is seven-and-aquarter acres, with a population of 2,028. I have described the Grotto Place (smaller) area and the King's Bench Walk area (excepting the small S.E. portion) in my Annual Report of 1897 (see pago 17). I have also several times suggested to your Health Committee the desirability of dealing with the Webber Row area as an unhealthy area under Part I. of the Working Classes Act, 1890. ] The vacant sites that appear to me to be immediately available in tho three Wards of your parish for building operations by the County Council represent a rough aggregate of 56,640 square feet. I would respectfully suggest to your Vestry that an official representation he made of the above unhealthy area and vacant sites without loss of time, to the L.C.C., who are the Local Authority under the Act, with a request that they will deal with the same forthwith under the resolutions already referred to as passed by the Council on the 29th November, 1898. From a perusal of the Act, 1 find that the official representation of the case must come through the Medical Officer of Health, and be addressed to the London County Council. In that event, a somewhat fuller statement of details would be required than has been possible in the short time available for the preparation of this Special Report, which I now have the honour of submitting to your Vestry. In the present instance, I have thought it would be better, in the interests of the parish, to submit the matter to the Vestry, in the first place, with a view of learning your views and wishes, and of determining the course of action most likely to secure |he approval of the ratepayers. 13th December, 1898. Annual Report or the Medical Officer of Health.—1898. 35 Special Report on Sewage Flooding in Basements. Amongst Bewage-flooded cellars visited by me were those in use as workrooms by bakers, cooks, printers, a butcher (a meat pickler), a window-blind maker, a urrier, a gas-fitter, a gilder, a paper-bag maker, and a publican. This question of basement-floodings in various low-lyings parts of the metropolis has been for some years past under the notice of the London County Council, and in 895 the Council resolved that the general enlargement of the main drainage system was not then necessary, but that "local flooding needed immediate attention." In 893—in accordance with the request of the Chairman of the Main Drainage Committee of the London County Council—I furnished particulars of 20 cellars in which found evidence of sewage flooding. A summary of a detailed report on the subject is recorded in my Annual Report to the Vestry for 1893. In this report I explained that "the special danger connected with this form of basement flooding is that this sewage is always more or less recharged with the specific poison of typhoid fever, and is at any time liable to that of cholera and of other deadly diseases. It is obvious that such contaminated cellars are unfitted for the preparation of bread or other articles of food, and almost equally 50 for use as workshops or dwelling rooms." I also added—"In reference to this report a correspondence took place between your Vestry and the London County Council. The latter, as you are aware, suggested that the less costly remedy would be to fill up the cellars rather than to enlarge the main sewer. No mention was made, however, of the question of compensation for disturbance to owners, nor did the Council state on whom it was proposed to lay the necessary cost involved of filling up the cellars. Further, no answer of any kind was given to my proposal contained in a previous report, that the Council should lay down a separate system of drainage for carrying off storm water." The London Building Act of 1891 provides against the occupancy in the future of dwelling-houses on low-lying land. Section 122 of this Act says:— "It shall not be lawful for any person upon land of which the surface is below the level of Trinity high-water mark, and which is so situate as not to admit of being drained by gravitation into an existing sewer of the Council, to erect any building to be used, wholly or in part, as a dwelling-house, or to adopt any building to be used, wholly or in part, as a dwelling-house, except with the permission of the Council, and subject to and in accordance with such regulations as the Council may from time to time prescribe with reference to the erection of buildings on such land." In concluding my Seventh Annual Report, I may again say, as in former years, that so long as I am honoured with the confidence of your Vestry, it will be my earnest endeavour to carry out the duties of the important office with which I have been entrusted, with all necessary care and vigilance. I have the honour to be, Your obedient Servant, F. J. WALDO, Medical Officer of Health. 1, Plowden Buildings, Temple, 21st March, 1899. 36 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark APPENDIX. Report of the Chief Sanitary Inspector. I beg to present the following report for the year ending the 31st December, 1898:- Daring the year 249 complaints have been made and entered in the official register kept for that purpose, being seven less than the preceding year. 5 notices in respect to 8 houses have been served under the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890. 22 inspections have been made relating to newly erected houses. 22 water supply certificates have also been granted, under section 48 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, relating to 22 houses and 12 tenements. 135 visits have been made relating to factories and workshops and 20 notices served in relation thereto, there are 369 now registered, 31 of which have been added during the year. Several alterations have been made so as to prevent the direct approach to the conveniences from any room used as a factory or workshop. This has been done by enclosing them by a solid wall or partition of brick or other materials, extending the entire height from the floor to the ceiling so as to screen the conveniences from view of the workers. The following is a copy of a memorandum forwarded me by the Local Government Board:— Memorandum as to Annual Reports of Medical Officers of Health. Every Medical Officer of Health appointed under Order of the Local Government Board is required to make an Annual Report with regard to the Sanitary district which 53 Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health—1898. is under his superintendence. The report is to be for the year ending the 31st December, or, if the Officer at that date has not been in office for a whole year, then for so much of the year as has elapsed since his appointment. The report is to be made to the Sanitary Authority, and the Medical Officer of Health himself should send a copy of it to the Local Government Board and to the London County Council. It should be made to the Sanitary Authority as soon as practicable after the expiration of the year to which it relates, and should be in the hands both of the Sanitary Authority and of the Local Government Board within at most five months from the end of the year. The Board's copy of the report should be forwarded to them when the original is sent to the Sanitary Authority, except where the report is likely to be printed by order of the Authority. In such cases the Board need only be supplied with a printed copy. Article 18 (Section 15) of the Board's Order of 8th December, 1891, specifies the information to be contained in the Annual Report, and is printed below. The report should be chiefly concerned with the conditions affecting health in the district and with the means of improving these conditions. It should consider these subjects with reference to the past and future, as well as to the particular year; and the account of the sanitary state of the district generally should, while marking the point that has been reached in the sanitary state and administration of the district, indicate directions for further consideration and action. The sanitary history of the year under review should include a record alike of the proceedings of the Medical Officer himself and of the proceedings taken under his direction or advice. The Medical Officer of Health, in reporting his proceedings and advice, may find it convenient to follow in the main the order in which the subject matters of his duty appear in the several paragraphs of Article 18. Special care should be taken to report fully and explicitly in reporting on the influences affecting or threatening to affect injuriously the public health within the district; and the causes, origin, and distribution of disease within the district may usefully be the subjects of annual record. An account should also be given both of the actual circumstances of the district up to the end of the past year and of any improvement or deterioration that has occurred during the year in conditions conducive to health or to disease. For example, a vigilant health officer has in his annual report an opportunity for pointing out any facts as to water supplies and house construction, conditions of storage and of removal of refuse customary in the district, together with any facts as to the adequacy or inadequacy of means of isolation and of disinfection, and the like; and such a health officer, reporting on the diseases and their causes within the district, will generally have some instructive details to give, either of conditions newly productive of disease or of matters that have been remedied with advantage to the public health. In reporting upon Section 3, not only should the fact of having made systematic inspections, but the outcome from those inspections should be duly put on record. The report should contain a full account of inspections, whether systematic or occasional, of the judgment which the Medical Officer of Health has formed thereon as to the sanitary state of his district, and of the advice he has in consequence given to the Sanitary Authority, and the action taken by the Authority thereon. By "systematic" inspections are meant inspections independent of such enquiries as, under other Article of the Order, the Medical Officer of Health has to make into particular outbreaks of disease, or into unwholesome conditions to which his attention may have been specially called by complaints or otherwise, and such inspections will include the house-to-house inspections which may be necessary in particular localities. In making systematic inspections, as in much of his other action, the Medical Officer of Health will usually have required the assistance of the Inspector of Nuisances, and it will bo for the Medical Officer to include in his report an account of the action which at his instance the Inspector may have taken for the removal of nuisances injurious to health, 54 Parish of St. George the Martyr, Southwark. The tabular statements of sickness and mortality in the district during the year, to be made on the forms supplied for the purpose, should be the subject of comment in the text of the report, in so far as deductions from them may assist the Sanitary Authority to an appreciation of the lines of action needful in the future. As regards these statements of sickness and mortality only one other observation appears to be needful. The district under the superintendence of a Medical Officer of Health will often contain several parts evidently differing in their circumstances, or having very different rates of mortality, either from all causes or of mortality from some particular disease or class of diseases. The observation of these differences can scarcely fail to lead to valuable information, and it is in view of such differences that the tabular statements are required in Section 15 to be classified according to localities, and that provision for such classification is made in the forms supplied for returns of deaths. In the absence of any ascertained differences of the above sort it will still be desirable to classify the deaths of the district according to the part of the district in which they occur; and for this purpose any areas of known population may be taken as representing "localities" for the purposes of the Order. Classification on this basis will be likely to lead to the discovery of real differences when the returns for several years can be compared together. The same considerations apply to the records of sickness (Table B). These records should be tabulated for every sanitary district, and it will be well before proceeding to fill the columns and the blank spaces to note carefully the headings and the foot notes. What has been said above with regard to the information which an annual report should contain must be understood not as suggesting that the report should be limited to these subjects or that more detailed or differently arranged tabular statements may not be added, but as indicating the sort of information required by the Board's Order. Many Medical Officers of Health will doubtless with great advantage to the administration of their districts furnish much more detailed information respecting particular questions to which they have been led by the circumstances of the foregoing years to devote attention, or in the investigation of which they may have arrived at valuable conclusions. Any information of this kind will be appreciated by the Local Government Board. (Signed) R. THORNE THORNE, Medical Officer. Local Government Office, December, 1896.
